<P align="center">
<B><FONT size="2">APPLIED MATERIALS, INC.</FONT></B>

<P align="center">
<B><FONT size="2">NOTES TO CONSOLIDATED CONDENSED FINANCIAL
STATEMENTS (Unaudited)</FONT></B>

<DIV align="center">
<B><FONT size="2">NINE MONTHS ENDED JULY 27, 2003</FONT></B>
</DIV>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">1)&nbsp;Basis of Presentation and Stock-Based
Compensation</FONT></B>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<I>Basis of
Presentation</I></FONT></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">In the opinion of management, the unaudited
interim consolidated condensed financial statements of Applied
Materials, Inc. (Applied) included herein have been prepared on
a basis consistent with the October&nbsp;27, 2002 audited
consolidated financial statements and include all material
adjustments, consisting of normal recurring adjustments,
necessary to fairly present the information set forth therein.
These unaudited interim consolidated condensed financial
statements should be read in conjunction with the audited
consolidated financial statements and notes thereto included in
Applied&#146;s Form&nbsp;10-K for the fiscal year ended
October&nbsp;27, 2002. Applied&#146;s results of operations for
the three and nine months ended July&nbsp;27, 2003 are not
necessarily indicative of future operating results.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">The preparation of financial statements in
conformity with generally accepted accounting principles in the
United States of America requires management to make estimates
and assumptions that affect the amounts reported in the
financial statements and accompanying notes. Actual results
could differ materially from those estimates.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Certain prior year amounts have been reclassified
to conform to the fiscal 2003 financial statement presentation.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">During the second fiscal quarter of 2003, Applied
reviewed the functional currencies of certain subsidiaries in
Europe and Japan and determined that the U.S.&nbsp;dollar was
more appropriate as the functional currency due to changes in
facts, circumstances, scope of operations and business
practices. The change in functional currencies did not have a
material effect on Applied&#146;s results of operations and
financial position for 2003.
</FONT>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<I>Stock-Based
Compensation</I></FONT></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">During the second fiscal quarter of 2003,
Statement of Financial Accounting Standards No.&nbsp;148
(SFAS&nbsp;148), &#147;Accounting for Stock-Based
Compensation&nbsp;&#151; Transition and Disclosure&nbsp;&#151;
An Amendment of FASB Statement&nbsp;No.&nbsp;123&#148; became
effective for Applied.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied measures compensation expense for its
stock-based employee compensation plans using the intrinsic
value method. As the exercise price of all options granted under
these plans was equal to the fair market price of the underlying
common stock on the grant date, no stock-based employee
compensation cost is recognized in the consolidated condensed
statements of operations.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">In accordance with SFAS&nbsp;148 and Statement of
Financial Accounting Standards No.&nbsp;123 (SFAS&nbsp;123),
&#147;Accounting for Stock-Based Compensation,&#148;
Applied&#146;s pro forma option expense is computed using the
Black-Scholes option pricing model. This model was developed for
use in estimating the value of publicly traded options that have
no vesting restrictions and are fully transferable.
Applied&#146;s employee stock options have characteristics
significantly different from those of traded options; therefore,
in the opinion of management, the Black-Scholes option pricing
model generally used to comply with SFAS&nbsp;148 and
SFAS&nbsp;123 does not necessarily provide a reliable measure of
the fair value of Applied&#146;s options.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">To comply with SFAS&nbsp;148, Applied is
presenting the following table to illustrate the effect on the
net income/(loss) and earnings/ (loss) per share if it had
applied the fair value recognition provisions of SFAS&nbsp;123,
as amended, to options granted under the stock-based employee
compensation plans. For purposes
</FONT>

<P align="center"><FONT size="2">5
</FONT>

</DIV>

<P align="left">
<FONT size="2">of this pro forma disclosure, the estimated value
of the options is amortized ratably to expense over the
options&#146; vesting periods.
</FONT>

    <TD width="3%"><FONT size="2">&nbsp;</FONT></TD>

    <TD width="3%"><FONT size="2">&nbsp;</FONT></TD>

    <TD width="1%"><FONT size="2">&nbsp;</FONT></TD>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Based on the Black-Scholes option pricing model,
the weighted average estimated fair value of employee stock
option grants was $10.87 and $7.41 for the three months ended
July&nbsp;28, 2002 and July&nbsp;27, 2003, respectively, and was
$10.87 and $6.80 for the nine months ended July&nbsp;28, 2002
and July&nbsp;27, 2003, respectively. The weighted average
estimated fair value of purchase rights granted under employee
stock purchase plans (ESPP)&nbsp;was $6.29 and $5.58 for the
third fiscal quarter ended July&nbsp;28, 2002 and July&nbsp;27,
2003, respectively. For the nine months ended July&nbsp;28, 2002
and July&nbsp;27, 2003, the weighted average estimated fair
value of purchase rights granted under ESPP was $6.29 and $5.24,
respectively. For purposes of this calculation, the fair value
of each stock option grant and stock purchase right is estimated
on the date of grant using the Black-Scholes option pricing
model and the following assumptions:

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">On May&nbsp;20, 2003, Applied issued
300,000&nbsp;shares of restricted common stock at $0.01&nbsp;per
share as part of the initial compensation package for the new
President and CEO. The closing market price of Applied&#146;s
common stock was $13.76&nbsp;per share on May&nbsp;20, 2003. One
half of the shares vest on October&nbsp;1, 2003, with the
remaining shares vesting on October&nbsp;1, 2004. The stock is
subject to forfeiture if employment terminates prior to vesting.
Deferred compensation was charged for the difference between the
market value of the restricted

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">2)&nbsp;Earnings/(Loss) Per Share</FONT></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Basic earnings/(loss) per share has been
determined using the weighted average number of common shares
outstanding during the period. Diluted earnings per share has
been determined using the weighted average number of common
shares and equivalents (representing the dilutive effect of
stock options) outstanding during the period. Applied&#146;s net
income/(loss) has not been adjusted for any period presented for
purposes of computing basic or diluted earnings/(loss) per share.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">For purposes of computing diluted earnings/(loss)
per share, weighted average common share equivalents do not
include stock options with an exercise price that exceeded the
average fair market value of Applied&#146;s common stock for the
period. For the three and nine months ended July&nbsp;28, 2002,
options to purchase approximately 24,156,000 and
22,154,000&nbsp;shares of common stock at an average exercise
price of $30.34 and $31.12 were excluded from the computation.
For the three and nine months ended July&nbsp;27, 2003, options
to purchase approximately 133,133,000 and
186,303,000&nbsp;shares of common stock at an average exercise
price of $21.34 and $19.58 were excluded from the computation.
</FONT>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">3)&nbsp;Accounts Receivable, Net</FONT></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied has agreements with various financial
institutions to sell accounts receivable from selected
customers. Applied sold accounts receivable under these
agreements of $252&nbsp;million and $130&nbsp;million for the
three months ended July&nbsp;28, 2002 and July&nbsp;27, 2003,
respectively, and sold $554&nbsp;million and $424&nbsp;million
for the nine months ended July&nbsp;28, 2002 and July&nbsp;27,
2003, respectively. Discounting fees were recorded as interest
expense and were insignificant for the three and nine months
ended July&nbsp;28, 2002 and July&nbsp;27, 2003. At
July&nbsp;27, 2003, $109&nbsp;million of sold receivables
remained outstanding under these agreements. A portion of these
sold receivables is subject to certain recourse provisions.
Applied has not experienced any losses under these recourse
provisions.
</FONT>

<FONT size="2">In connection with the adoption of Statement of
Financial Accounting Standards No.&nbsp;142, &#147;Goodwill and
Other Intangible Assets,&#148; as of the beginning of fiscal
2002, goodwill is no longer amortized but reviewed periodically
for impairment. From October&nbsp;27, 2002 to July&nbsp;27,
2003, the change in goodwill was $21&nbsp;million, which
primarily consisted of $18&nbsp;million for the acquisition of
Boxer Cross, Inc. and a $3&nbsp;million adjustment to the
purchase price for an acquisition containing contingent purchase
price provisions. For additional details of the acquisition of
Boxer Cross, Inc. in the second fiscal quarter of 2003, see
Note&nbsp;10 to the Consolidated Condensed Financial Statements.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Purchased technology and other intangible assets
are amortized over their estimated useful lives of five to
10&nbsp;years using the straight-line method. Aggregate
amortization expense was $39&nbsp;million and $36&nbsp;million
for the nine months ended July&nbsp;28, 2002 and July&nbsp;27,
2003, respectively. As of July&nbsp;27, 2003, future estimated
amortization expense is expected to be $12&nbsp;million for the
remainder of fiscal 2003, $48&nbsp;million for fiscal 2004,
$19&nbsp;million for fiscal 2005, $13&nbsp;million for fiscal
2006, $5&nbsp;million for fiscal 2007 and $5&nbsp;million
thereafter.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied has a venture capital fund, Applied
Materials Ventures&nbsp;I, L.P. (Ventures&nbsp;I), that invests
in privately-held, early-stage companies engaged in developing
systems, components and devices based on nanotechnology for
specific applications and products. Ventures&nbsp;I is a limited
partnership with Applied as the sole limited partner and an
independent party as the general partner. Applied has committed
to fund $50&nbsp;million in capital contributions, but has
reserved the option to discontinue capital contributions at
$25&nbsp;million. Applied&#146;s capital contributions to
Ventures&nbsp;I totaled approximately $9&nbsp;million at
October&nbsp;27, 2002 and $15&nbsp;million at July&nbsp;27,
2003. As provided for in the partnership agreement, the general
partner has control over investment decisions and operations of
Ventures&nbsp;I. Accordingly, Applied accounts for its
investment using the equity method. Capital contributions, net
of the pro rata share of Ventures&nbsp;I&#146;s results of
operations, have been included in other assets and totaled
$6&nbsp;million at October&nbsp;27, 2002 and $10&nbsp;million at
July&nbsp;27, 2003. Applied does not expect its investment in
Ventures&nbsp;I to have a material effect on its financial
condition or results of operation.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">In January 2003, the Financial Accounting
Standard Board (FASB)&nbsp;issued Interpretation No.&nbsp;46
(FIN&nbsp;46), &#147;Consolidation of Variable Interest
Entities, an interpretation of ARB No.&nbsp;51.&#148;
FIN&nbsp;46 provides guidance on the identification,
classification and accounting of variable interest entities.
Applied has determined that Ventures&nbsp;I qualifies for
consolidation as a variable interest entity under FIN&nbsp;46.
Applied will
</FONT>

<P align="center"><FONT size="2">8
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied adopted FASB Interpretation No.&nbsp;45
(FIN&nbsp;45), &#147;Guarantor&#146;s Accounting and Disclosure
Requirements for Guarantees, including Indirect Indebtedness of
Others,&#148; during the first fiscal quarter of 2003.
FIN&nbsp;45 requires disclosures concerning Applied&#146;s
obligations under certain guarantees.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Pursuant to FIN&nbsp;45, Applied is required to
disclose product warranty reserves activity. Applied products
are generally sold with a 12-month warranty period following
installation. Parts and labor are covered under the terms of the
warranty agreement. The warranty provision is based on
historical experience by product, configuration and geographic
region.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">As noted above, Applied&#146;s products are
generally sold with a 12-month warranty. Accordingly, quarterly
warranty consumption is associated with sales that occurred
during the preceding four quarters, and quarterly warranty
provisions are generally related to the current quarter&#146;s
sales.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">During the ordinary course of business, Applied
also provides standby letters of credit or other guarantee
instruments to certain parties as required for certain
transactions initiated by either Applied or its subsidiaries. As
of July&nbsp;27, 2003, the maximum potential amount of future
payments that Applied could be required to make under these
guarantee agreements is approximately $48&nbsp;million. Applied
has not recorded any liability in
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied also has additional guarantee
arrangements on behalf of certain subsidiaries. As of
July&nbsp;27, 2003, Applied has not recorded any liability
related to guarantees of subsidiary obligations. Applied does
not expect, based on historical experience and information
currently available, that it is probable any amounts will be
required to be paid under these arrangements. Subsidiary
guarantees as of July&nbsp;27, 2003 were associated with the
following types of arrangements: short-term borrowings, term
loans, overdrafts and leases. While certain subsidiaries have
short-term borrowing, term loans and overdraft facilities
available totaling approximately $160 million as of
July&nbsp;27, 2003, no amounts were outstanding as of the end of
the third fiscal quarter of 2003. In the event of use and
subsequent default of these facilities by Applied&#146;s
subsidiaries, such arrangements would be guaranteed by Applied.
In addition, certain subsidiaries have lease arrangements
guaranteed by Applied. These leases will expire between 2009 and
2014. In the event that the subsidiaries do not make the
required payments, Applied could be required to pay the leases
on behalf of the subsidiaries. As of July&nbsp;27, 2003,
quarterly lease obligations under these arrangements
approximated $3&nbsp;million.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">In connection with certain business combinations
and purchased technology acquisitions, Applied was subject to
contingent consideration arrangements at July&nbsp;27, 2003.
These arrangements are based upon sales volume or other events
taking place subsequent to the acquisition. Depending upon the
circumstances of each agreement, the payment of the contingency
will result in an increase to goodwill or a charge to operating
expenses. Amounts paid under these arrangements have not had,
and are not expected to have, a material effect on
Applied&#146;s financial condition or results of operations.
</FONT>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">7)&nbsp;Restructuring, Asset Impairments and
Other Charges</FONT></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">In response to continuing difficult business
conditions, Applied implemented a series of restructuring
actions. These actions were intended to better align
Applied&#146;s cost structure with prevailing economic
conditions.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">During the third fiscal quarter of 2003, Applied
continued to execute restructuring actions, resulting in
restructuring, asset impairments and other pre-tax charges of
$66&nbsp;million, or $0.03 per share after tax. Consequently,
Applied reduced its global workforce by approximately
450&nbsp;positions or three percent. The majority of the
affected employees were based in North America, Asia and Europe,
and represented multiple company activities and functions. The
restructuring, asset impairments and other charges of
$66&nbsp;million during the third fiscal quarter of 2003
included approximately $26&nbsp;million of non-cash items,
primarily for fixed asset write-offs. The majority of the cash
outlays have been incurred or are anticipated to occur in fiscal
2003.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">During the second fiscal quarter of 2003, Applied
began to execute restructuring actions to align its
infrastructure with current business conditions, resulting in
restructuring, asset impairments and other pre-tax charges of
$93&nbsp;million, or $0.04 per share after tax. Applied reduced
its global workforce by approximately 1,400&nbsp;positions or
10&nbsp;percent. The majority of the affected employees were
based in Santa Clara, California and Austin, Texas and
represented multiple company activities and functions. The
restructuring, asset impairments and other charges of
$93&nbsp;million during the second fiscal quarter of 2003
included approximately $30&nbsp;million of non-cash items,
primarily for fixed asset write-offs. The majority of the cash
outlays have been incurred or are anticipated to occur in fiscal
2003.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">During the first fiscal quarter of 2003, Applied
recorded a pre-tax restructuring charge of $99&nbsp;million, or
$0.04 per share after tax, for employee-related costs and
consolidation of facilities. As a result of this action,
</FONT>

facilities and other costs, and reduced its global workforce by
approximately 1,100&nbsp;positions, or six percent. The majority
of the affected employees were based in Santa Clara, California
and Austin, Texas, and represented multiple company activities
and functions. The restructuring charge of $77&nbsp;million
consisted of $49&nbsp;million of cash outlays and

write-offs.

<FONT size="2">At July&nbsp;27, 2003, the restructuring reserve
consisted of $6&nbsp;million related to a restructuring
implemented in the fourth fiscal quarter of 2001,
$7&nbsp;million related to the restructuring implemented in the
first fiscal quarter of 2002, $15&nbsp;million related to the
restructuring implemented in the first fiscal quarter of 2003,
$3&nbsp;million related to the restructuring implemented in the
second fiscal quarter of 2003 and $33&nbsp;million related to
the restructuring implemented the third fiscal quarter of 2003.
Restructuring reserves associated with actions initiated in
prior years were mainly for lease commitments.
</FONT>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">8)&nbsp;Derivative Financial
Instruments</FONT></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">In accordance with Statement of Financial
Accounting Standards No.&nbsp;133 (SFAS&nbsp;133),
&#147;Accounting for Derivative Instruments and Hedging
Activities,&#148; as amended, all of Applied&#146;s derivative
financial instruments, consisting of forward exchange contracts
and currency option contracts such as Japanese yen, euro and
British pounds are recorded at their fair value on the balance
sheet, either in other current assets or accounts payable and
accrued expenses. Changes in the fair value of derivatives that
do not qualify for hedge treatment, as well as the ineffective
portion of any hedges, must be recognized currently in results
of operations. The effective
</FONT>

</FONT>

<P align="left">
<FONT size="2">portion of the gain or loss is reported as a
component of accumulated other comprehensive income in
stockholders&#146; equity, and is reclassified into results of
operations when the hedged transaction affects earnings/(loss).
All amounts included in accumulated other comprehensive income
at July&nbsp;27, 2003 will be reclassified to results of
operations within 12&nbsp;months. Changes in the fair value of
currency option contracts due to changes in time value are
excluded from the assessment of effectiveness, and are
recognized in cost of products sold. The change in option time
value was not material for the three and nine months ended
July&nbsp;28, 2002 and July&nbsp;27, 2003. If the transaction
being hedged fails to occur, or if a portion of any derivative
is ineffective, Applied immediately recognizes the gain or loss
on the associated financial instrument in general and
administrative expenses. The amounts recognized due to the
anticipated transactions failing to occur were not material for
the three and nine months ended July&nbsp;28, 2002 and
July&nbsp;27, 2003.
</FONT>

<FONT size="2">Derivative-related activity in accumulated other
comprehensive income for the nine months ended July&nbsp;27,
2003 decreased by $5&nbsp;million, which was comprised of a
$3&nbsp;million net increase in fair value of derivatives,
offset by $8&nbsp;million of net gains which were reclassified
from accumulated other comprehensive income to results of
operations.

stock-based employee benefit plans. Upon the expiration of the
previous authorization on March&nbsp;22, 2001,
</FONT>

market over the succeeding three years. Under this
authorization, Applied is continuing a systematic stock
repurchase program and may also make additional stock
repurchases from time to time, depending on market conditions,
stock price and other factors.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">During the nine months ended July&nbsp;28, 2002,
Applied repurchased 3,194,000 shares of its common stock at an
average price of $21.86 for a cash outlay of $70&nbsp;million.
During the nine months ended July&nbsp;27, 2003, Applied
repurchased 12,019,000 shares of its common stock at an average
price of $14.55, for a total cash outlay of $175&nbsp;million.
</FONT>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">10)&nbsp;Business Combinations</FONT></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">On April&nbsp;18, 2003, Applied acquired Boxer
Cross, Inc., a producer of in-line monitoring systems that
provide customers with critical electrical measurement data for
controlling semiconductor processes, for $14&nbsp;million in
cash. In connection with this acquisition, Applied recorded
goodwill of $18&nbsp;million, net of adjustments to the initial
purchase price allocation, and purchased technology of
$3&nbsp;million, partially offset by other items of
$7&nbsp;million, primarily for deferred tax assets and other
liabilities. The in-process research and development expense was
insignificant.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">On April&nbsp;8, 2002, Applied acquired Electron
Vision Corporation, a designer, manufacturer and seller of
e-beam stabilization and curing tools for the semiconductor,
thin film head and micro-fabrication industries, for
$26&nbsp;million in cash. In connection with this acquisition,
Applied recorded goodwill of $13&nbsp;million, net of
adjustments to the initial purchase price allocation, and
purchased technology of $16&nbsp;million, partially offset by
other items of $3 million, primarily for deferred tax
liabilities.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">On December&nbsp;3, 2001, Applied acquired GKS, a
provider of advanced data mining services to improve
semiconductor manufacturing yield and efficiency, for
$16&nbsp;million in cash. In connection with this acquisition,
Applied recorded acquired in-process research and development
expense of $2&nbsp;million, goodwill of $6&nbsp;million,
purchased technology of $4&nbsp;million and other items of
$4&nbsp;million. The amount of acquired in-process research and
development expense was determined by identifying research
projects for which technological feasibility had not been
established and for which no alternative future use existed. The
value of the projects identified as in-process was determined by
estimating the future cash flows from the projects once
commercially feasible, discounting the net cash flows back to
their present value at a rate commensurate with the level of
risk and maturity of the projects, and then applying a
percentage-of-completion to the calculated value.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">On November&nbsp;20, 2001, Applied acquired the
assets of Schlumberger&#146;s electron-beam wafer inspection
business for $66&nbsp;million in cash. In connection with this
acquisition, Applied recorded acquired in-process research and
development expense of $6&nbsp;million and goodwill of $81
million, net of adjustments to the initial purchase price
allocation, partially offset by net liabilities acquired of
$21&nbsp;million. The amount of acquired in-process research and
development expense was determined by identifying research
projects for which technological feasibility had not been
established and for which no alternative future use existed. The
value of the projects identified as in-process was determined by
calculating the total development costs incurred, estimating the
portion of development costs related to the aspect of the
project that Applied expected to utilize, and then calculating
the current value of these historical development costs using a
Consumer Price Index adjustment.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">For all of the purchase business combinations
discussed above, the results of operations prior to the
acquisition dates were not material in relation to those of
Applied for any of the periods presented herein. Goodwill is not
amortized but is reviewed periodically for impairment, and
purchased technology is amortized over its useful life of five
to 10&nbsp;years. These acquisitions have not had, and are not
expected to have, a material effect on Applied&#146;s financial
condition or results of operations.
</FONT>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">11)&nbsp;New Accounting
Pronouncements</FONT></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">In April 2002, the FASB issued Statement of
Financial Accounting Standards (SFAS)&nbsp;No.&nbsp;145
(SFAS&nbsp;145), &#147;Rescission of FASB Statements No.&nbsp;4,
44 and 64, Amendment of FASB Statement No.&nbsp;13, and
Technical Corrections.&#148; SFAS&nbsp;145 eliminates
SFAS&nbsp;4, &#147;Reporting Gains and Losses from
Extinguishment of Debt,&#148; which required all gains and
losses from extinguishment of debt to be aggregated and, if
material, classified as an extraordinary item. Under
SFAS&nbsp;145, such gains and losses should be classified as
extraordinary only if they meet the criteria of APB Opinion
No.&nbsp;30. In addition, SFAS&nbsp;145 amends SFAS&nbsp;13,
&#147;Accounting for Leases,&#148; to eliminate an inconsistency
between the required accounting for sale-leaseback transactions
and the required accounting for certain lease modifications that
have economic effects that are similar to sale-leaseback
transactions. Applied does not expect the adoption of
SFAS&nbsp;145, which will become effective at varying dates from
May 2002 to the end of Applied&#146;s fiscal 2003, to have a
material effect on its financial condition or results of
operations.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">In November 2002, the Emerging Issues Task Force
reached a consensus on Issue No.&nbsp;00-21 (EITF&nbsp;00-21),
&#147;Revenue Arrangements with Multiple Deliverables.&#148;
EITF&nbsp;00-21 provides guidance on accounting for arrangements
that involve the delivery of performance of multiple products,
services and/or rights to use assets. Applied does not expect
the adoption of EITF&nbsp;00-21, which will apply to revenue
arrangements beginning in Applied&#146;s fourth fiscal quarter
of 2003, to have a material effect on its financial condition or
results of operations.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">In January 2003, the FASB issued FASB
Interpretation No.&nbsp;46 (FIN&nbsp;46), &#147;Consolidation of
Variable Interest Entities, an interpretation of
ARB&nbsp;No.&nbsp;51.&#148; FIN&nbsp;46 provides guidance on:
1)&nbsp;the identification of entities for which control is
achieved through means other than through voting rights, known
as &#147;variable interest entities&#148; (VIEs); and
2)&nbsp;which business enterprise is the primary beneficiary and
when it should consolidate the VIE. This new model for
consolidation applies to entities: 1)&nbsp;where the equity
investors (if any) do not have a controlling financial interest;
or 2)&nbsp;whose equity investment at risk is insufficient to
finance that entity&#146;s activities without receiving
additional subordinated financial support from other parties. In
addition, FIN&nbsp;46 requires that both the primary beneficiary
and all other enterprises with a significant variable interest
in a VIE make additional disclosures. FIN&nbsp;46 is effective
for all new VIEs created or acquired after January&nbsp;31,
2003. For VIEs created or acquired prior to February&nbsp;1,
2003, the provisions of FIN&nbsp;46 must be applied for the
first interim or annual period beginning after June&nbsp;15,
2003. Certain disclosures are effective immediately. Applied
will implement FIN&nbsp;46 during the fourth fiscal quarter of
2003 and will consolidate Applied Ventures I as a VIE. Applied
does not expect its implementation to have a material effect on
its financial condition or results of operations. For additional
discussion of the Ventures&nbsp;I, see Note&nbsp;5 of the Notes
to the Consolidated Condensed Financial Statements.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">In April 2003, the FASB issued Statement of
Financial Accounting Standards No.&nbsp;149 (SFAS&nbsp;149),
&#147;Amendment of Statement&nbsp;133 on Derivative Instruments
and Hedging Activities.&#148; This statement amends
SFAS&nbsp;133 to provide clarification on the financial
accounting and reporting of derivative instruments and hedging
activities and requires contracts with similar characteristics
to be accounted for on a comparable basis. Applied does not
expect the adoption of SFAS&nbsp;149, which will be effective
for contracts entered into or modified after June&nbsp;30, 2003,
to have a material effect on its financial condition or results
of operations.
</FONT>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">Results of Operations</FONT></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied is a supplier of semiconductor
manufacturing equipment and services to the global semiconductor
industry. Business activity in the semiconductor and
semiconductor manufacturing equipment industries has been
cyclical. For this and other reasons, Applied&#146;s results of
operations for the three and nine months ended July&nbsp;27,
2003 may not necessarily be indicative of future operating
results.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied received new orders of $1.1&nbsp;billion
for the third fiscal quarter of 2003, compared to
$971&nbsp;million for the second fiscal quarter of 2003 and
$1.8&nbsp;billion for the third fiscal quarter of 2002. The
increase from the second fiscal quarter of 2003 reflected
gradual improvement of global economic conditions, increased
demand for DRAMs, increased customer fab utilization for
advanced integrated circuit production and increased 300mm
demand.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Orders for the third fiscal quarter of 2003 were
lower than the comparable period in 2002. During the third
fiscal quarter of 2002, Applied&#146;s customers reacted to an
increase in demand for logic integrated circuits by increasing
their orders for capital equipment in an effort to increase
capacity. When the anticipated business did not meet customer
expectations, these customers reduced or deferred capital
equipment spending from the fourth fiscal quarter of 2002
through the second fiscal quarter of 2003.
</FONT>

<FONT size="2">New orders by region for the past two consecutive
quarters were as follows (dollars in millions):

<!-- PAGEBREAK -->
<P><HR noshade><P>
<H5 align="left" style="page-break-before:always"><A HREF="#toc">Table of Contents</A></H5><P>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Orders in Taiwan were significantly higher for
the third fiscal quarter of 2003 as certain customers began to
place orders against their previously announced capital
expansion plans. Orders in Japan and Europe decreased due to
relatively stronger order volumes experienced in the second
fiscal quarter for these two regions. Orders in the other
regions were consistent with prior quarters.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied&#146;s backlog for the most recent three
quarters was as follows: $2.5&nbsp;billion at July&nbsp;27,
2003, $2.8&nbsp;billion at April&nbsp;27, 2003 and $3.1 billion
at January&nbsp;26, 2003. Backlog consists only of orders for
which written authorizations have been accepted, shipment dates
within 12&nbsp;months have been assigned and revenue has not
been recognized. Due to possible customer changes in delivery
schedules and cancellations of orders, Applied&#146;s backlog at
any particular date is not necessarily indicative of actual
sales for any future periods.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">During fiscal 2002, net sales remained relatively
flat for the first and second fiscal quarters, followed by a
modest increase in the third fiscal quarter. In the fourth
fiscal quarter of 2002, net sales declined slightly, decreased
further in the first fiscal quarter of 2003, and remained at
approximately $1.1&nbsp;billion for the second and third fiscal
quarters of 2003. Net sales for the nine months ended
July&nbsp;27, 2003 were 10&nbsp;percent lower than the nine
months ended July&nbsp;28, 2002. The overall sales level in
fiscal 2003 was lower than fiscal 2002, reflecting the impact of
the continuing industry downturn. Net sales by region for the
three months and nine months ended July&nbsp;28, 2002 and
July&nbsp;27, 2003 were as follows (dollars in millions):
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">The table above compares the percentages of net
sales for the three and nine months ended July&nbsp;28, 2002 to
the three and nine months ended July&nbsp;27, 2003. North
America increased as certain customers qualified and authorized
the delivery of the pilot equipment for 90&nbsp;nanometers.
Europe increased due to higher shipments to new 300mm fabs.
Japan increased due to improved fab utilization. Taiwan
decreased due to a significant reduction in foundry and DRAM
business. Korea and Asia-Pacific remained consistent with the
prior year.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">In response to continuing difficult business
conditions, Applied implemented a series of activities to better
align Applied&#146;s cost structure with prevailing economic
conditions. Realignment activities consisted of refocused
product development, cost reduction programs and restructuring
actions. To date, realignment activities consisted of refocused
product efforts, including refocusing the electron-beam mask
pattern product line, completing the final stage of the
implementation of the global spares distribution system (which
included closure of a central warehouse), consolidation of
facilities and reductions in workforce. Realignment activities
resulted in charges across multiple categories, as incurred,
such as cost of products sold and operating expenses. The
charges for the three months and nine months ended July&nbsp;27,
2003 were incurred across the following categories:
restructuring, asset impairments and other charges of
$66&nbsp;million and $258&nbsp;million, respectively; costs of
products sold of $93&nbsp;million and $142&nbsp;million,
respectively; and research, development and engineering expense
of $4&nbsp;million and $14&nbsp;million respectively.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Gross margin was 31.7&nbsp;percent for the third
fiscal quarter of 2003, compared to 33.7&nbsp;percent for the
second fiscal quarter of 2003 and 41.5&nbsp;percent for the
third fiscal quarter of 2002. Gross margin was 34.1&nbsp;percent
for the nine months ended July&nbsp;27, 2003 compared to
40.2&nbsp;percent for the nine&nbsp;months ended
</FONT>

<P align="center"><FONT size="2">16
</FONT>
<!-- PAGEBREAK -->
<P><HR noshade><P>
<H5 align="left" style="page-break-before:always"><A HREF="#toc">Table of Contents</A></H5><P>

<DIV align="left">
<FONT size="2">July&nbsp;28, 2002. The decrease in gross margin
during fiscal 2003 was mainly due to charges incurred from the
refocused product programs, including refocusing the
electron-beam mask pattern product line, and implementation of
the global spares distribution system.
</FONT>
</DIV>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Operating expenses included expenses related to
research, development and engineering (RD&#38;E), marketing and
selling (M&#38;S), general and administrative (G&#38;A), and
restructuring, asset impairments and other charges. Expenses
related to RD&#38;E, M&#38;S and G&#38;A were $367&nbsp;million
for the third fiscal quarter of 2003, compared to
$394&nbsp;million for the second fiscal quarter of 2003 and
$470&nbsp;million for the third fiscal quarter of 2002. These
expenses were $1.2&nbsp;billion for the nine months ended
July&nbsp;27, 2003, versus $1.3&nbsp;billion for the comparable
period of fiscal 2002. The reduction in these expenses was
attributable to ongoing cost control measures, reduction of
discretionary spending and savings resulting from realignment
activities.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Operating expenses also included restructuring,
asset impairments and other charges, which for the third fiscal
quarter of 2003 totaled $66&nbsp;million. Restructuring, asset
impairments and other charges were $258&nbsp;million and
$85&nbsp;million for the nine months ended July&nbsp;27, 2003
and July&nbsp;28, 2002, respectively. These charges consisted of
costs associated with reductions in workforce and consolidation
of facilities. For further details, see Note&nbsp;7 of Notes to
Consolidated Condensed Financial Statements.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Net interest income was $27&nbsp;million and
$35&nbsp;million for the three months ended July&nbsp;28, 2002
and July&nbsp;27, 2003, respectively, and was $98&nbsp;million
and $85&nbsp;million for the nine months ended July&nbsp;28,
2002 and July&nbsp;27, 2003, respectively. Higher net interest
income during the third fiscal quarter of 2003 was due to gains
realized from the sale of certain investments during the
quarter. Lower net interest income for the nine months ended
July&nbsp;27, 2003 was principally attributable to lower average
interest rates during fiscal 2003.
</FONT>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">Financial Condition, Liquidity and Capital
Resources</FONT></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">During the first three quarters of 2003, Applied
increased its cash, cash equivalents and short-term investments
by $303&nbsp;million from October&nbsp;27, 2002 to
$5.2&nbsp;billion at July&nbsp;27, 2003.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied generated cash from operating activities
of $537&nbsp;million for the nine months ended July&nbsp;27,
2003. The primary sources of cash were from reductions in
accounts receivable and inventories, which were offset by a
decrease in payables and the net loss for the nine months ended
July&nbsp;27, 2003. Applied utilized programs to sell accounts
receivable of $424&nbsp;million for the nine months ended
July&nbsp;27, 2003. These receivable sales had the effect of
increasing cash and reducing accounts receivable. Applied has
not experienced any losses under these programs. Days sales
outstanding was 62&nbsp;days at the end of the third fiscal
quarter of 2003, compared to 61&nbsp;days at the end of the
second fiscal quarter of 2003. For further details regarding
accounts receivable sales, see Note&nbsp;3 of Notes to
Consolidated Condensed Financial Statements. Inventories were
reduced by $284&nbsp;million during the nine months ended
July&nbsp;27, 2003, due primarily to ongoing inventory
management and reduction programs, and inventory charges related
to realignment activities.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied used $483&nbsp;million of cash for
investing activities during the nine months ended July&nbsp;27,
2003. Capital expenditures were $193&nbsp;million,
$52&nbsp;million of which was for the purchase of facilities in
Hillsboro, Oregon that were previously held under a synthetic
lease. The remainder of the capital spending consisted of
expenditures for application laboratories, equipment and related
facilities. Investing activities also included purchases and
sales of short-term investments, which used $315&nbsp;million of
cash.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied used $54&nbsp;million of cash for
financing activities for the nine months ended July&nbsp;27,
2003, consisting of stock repurchases and repayments of debt,
offset by issuances of common stock under employee stock plans.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Although cash requirements will fluctuate based
on the timing and extent of many factors, Applied&#146;s
management believes that cash generated from operations,
together with the liquidity provided by existing cash balances
and borrowing capability, will be sufficient to satisfy
Applied&#146;s liquidity requirements for the next
12&nbsp;months. For further details regarding Applied&#146;s
operating, investing and financing activities see the
Consolidated Condensed Statements of Cash Flows.
</FONT>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">Critical Accounting Policies</FONT></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">The preparation of financial statements and
related disclosures in conformity with accounting principles
generally accepted in the United States of America requires
management to make judgments, assumptions and estimates that
affect the amounts reported. Please see the discussion of
critical accounting policies in Applied&#146;s Annual Report on
Form&nbsp;10-K for the fiscal year ended October&nbsp;27, 2002.
</FONT>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">Trends, Risks and Uncertainties</FONT></B>

<P align="left">
<B><I><FONT size="2">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The industry
that Applied serves is highly volatile and
unpredictable.</FONT></I></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">As a supplier to the global semiconductor
industry, Applied is subject to the industry&#146;s business
cycles, the timing, length and volatility of which are difficult
to predict. The semiconductor industry has historically been
cyclical because of sudden changes in demand for semiconductors
and manufacturing capacity, including capacity utilizing the
latest technology. The rate of changes in demand, including end
demand, is accelerating, and the effect of these changes on
Applied is occurring sooner, exacerbating the volatility of
these cycles. These changes have affected the timing and amounts
of customers&#146; capital equipment purchases and investments
in new technology, and continue to affect Applied&#146;s net
sales, gross margin and results of operations. In addition to
affecting Applied&#146;s customers and suppliers, these business
cycles also challenge key management, engineering and other
employees of Applied.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">If Applied is unable to effectively manage its
resources and production capacity during periods of changing
demand, there could be a material adverse effect on its
business, financial condition and results of operations. During
periods of declining demand, customers typically reduce
purchases, delay delivery of products and/or cancel orders.
During downturns, Applied must be able to appropriately align
its cost structure with prevailing market conditions,
successfully manage its resources, and effectively motivate and
retain key employees. In response to a prolonged downturn,
Applied is implementing realignment activities in order to align
its cost structure with business conditions and to enable
Applied to focus resources on core research and development
programs. If Applied is unable to implement these realignment
activities according to the timetable and to the extent
anticipated, if implementation negatively affects Applied&#146;s
net sales or profitability, or if Applied does not maintain
effective cost controls, Applied&#146;s business, financial
condition, or results of operations may be negatively affected.
Conversely, during periods of growing demand for semiconductor
manufacturing equipment, Applied must be able to acquire and/or
develop sufficient manufacturing capacity and inventory to meet
customer demand, and to attract, hire, assimilate and retain a
sufficient number of qualified individuals. Management cannot
predict the timing of a recovery and/or the industry&#146;s rate
of growth in such a recovery, both of which will be affected by
many factors, including the global uncertainties discussed below.
</FONT>

<P align="left">
<B><I><FONT size="2">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Applied is
exposed to the risks of operating a global
business.</FONT></I></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">For the first nine months of fiscal 2003,
approximately 70&nbsp;percent of Applied&#146;s net sales were
to regions outside the United States. Certain manufacturing
facilities and suppliers of Applied are also located outside the
United States. Managing Applied&#146;s global operations
presents challenges, including those arising from periodic
regional economic downturns, trade balance issues, varying
business conditions and demands, variations in enforcement of
intellectual property and contract rights in different
jurisdictions, differences in the ability to develop
relationships with suppliers and other local businesses, changes
in U.S. and international laws and regulations including
U.S.&nbsp;export restrictions, fluctuations in interest and
currency exchange rates, the need to provide sufficient levels
of technical support in different locations, cultural
differences and shipping delays, among other risks. Many of
these challenges are present in China, a large potential market
for semiconductor equipment and an area that Applied anticipates
will present a significant opportunity for growth. These
challenges, as well as global uncertainties with respect to:
1)&nbsp;economic growth rates in various countries;
2)&nbsp;sustainability of demand for electronics products;
3)&nbsp;capital spending by semiconductor manufacturers;
4)&nbsp;price weakness for certain semiconductor devices; and
5)&nbsp;political instability, terrorism, acts of war, or
epidemics in regions where Applied has operations or sales,
including Asia and Israel, may affect Applied&#146;s business,
financial condition and results of operations.
</FONT>

<P align="center"><FONT size="2">18
</FONT>

<!-- PAGEBREAK -->
<P><HR noshade><P>
<H5 align="left" style="page-break-before:always"><A HREF="#toc">Table of Contents</A></H5><P>

<DIV>&nbsp;</DIV>

<TABLE width="100%" border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0">

<TR>
    <TD width="3%"></TD>
    <TD width="97%"></TD>
</TR>

<TR valign="top">
    <TD></TD>
    <TD>
    <B><I><FONT size="2">Applied operates in a highly competitive
    industry characterized by increasingly rapid technological
    changes.</FONT></I></B></TD>
</TR>

</TABLE>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Because it operates in a highly competitive
environment, Applied&#146;s future success is heavily dependent
upon effective development, commercialization and customer
acceptance of its new products over those of its competitors.
Specifically, these risks may include, but are not limited to,
Applied&#146;s ability to timely and cost-effectively:
1)&nbsp;develop new products, services and technologies,
including those utilizing new materials, such as copper and
low-k materials; 2)&nbsp;develop improvements to existing
products, services and technologies; 3)&nbsp;develop new markets
in the semiconductor industry for Applied&#146;s products and
services; 4)&nbsp;introduce new products and services to the
marketplace; 5)&nbsp;achieve market acceptance and accurately
forecast product demand; 6)&nbsp;transition from 200mm systems
to 300mm systems; 7)&nbsp;qualify new or improved products for
volume manufacturing with its customers; 8)&nbsp;commence and
adjust production to meet customer demands; 9)&nbsp;price
products and services appropriately; and 10)&nbsp;lower
customers&#146; cost of ownership. The development, introduction
and support of an increasingly broad set of new or improved
products and technologies, including those enabling the
transition to smaller device feature sizes, new materials and
300mm wafers, grow increasingly complex and expensive over time.
Such new or improved products may involve higher costs and
reduced efficiencies compared to Applied&#146;s more established
products and could adversely affect Applied&#146;s gross
margins. If Applied does not develop and introduce new or
improved products, services and technologies in a timely and
cost-effective manner in response to changing market conditions
or customer requirements, its competitive position, financial
condition and results of operations could be materially and
adversely affected.
</FONT>

<DIV>&nbsp;</DIV>

<TABLE width="100%" border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0">

<TR>
    <TD width="3%"></TD>
    <TD width="97%"></TD>
</TR>

<TR valign="top">
    <TD></TD>
    <TD>
    <B><I><FONT size="2">Applied is exposed to risks associated with
    a highly concentrated customer base.</FONT></I></B></TD>
</TR>

</TABLE>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied&#146;s customer base is highly
concentrated. Orders from a relatively limited number of
manufacturers of integrated circuits have accounted for, and
likely will continue to account for, a substantial portion of
Applied&#146;s net sales, which may lead customers to demand
pricing and other terms less favorable to Applied. In addition,
sales to any single customer may vary significantly from quarter
to quarter. If current customers delay, cancel or do not place
orders, Applied may not be able to replace these orders with new
orders. As Applied&#146;s products are configured to customer
specifications, changing, rescheduling or canceling orders may
result in significant and often non-recoverable costs. The
resulting fluctuations in the amount of and terms of orders
could have a material adverse effect on Applied&#146;s business,
financial condition and results of operations.
</FONT>

<DIV>&nbsp;</DIV>

<TABLE width="100%" border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0">

<TR>
    <TD width="3%"></TD>
    <TD width="97%"></TD>
</TR>

<TR valign="top">
    <TD></TD>
    <TD>
    <B><I><FONT size="2">Applied is exposed to risks as a result of
    ongoing changes in the semiconductor industry.</FONT></I></B></TD>
</TR>

</TABLE>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">The semiconductor industry is characterized by
rapid ongoing changes, including more complex technology
requirements, the changing information technology cost
structure, the increasing significance of consumer electronics
as a driver for chip demand and the related focus on lower
prices, and the growing type and variety of integrated circuits
and applications. There also is an increasing number of
applications in the semiconductor industry, resulting in
changing interests among semiconductor manufacturers, as well as
a rising percentage of business from customers in Asia, an
emergence of customers in new geographical regions, and higher
capital requirements for new semiconductor fabrication plants.
These factors are increasing the need for customer partnering,
collective research and development efforts and process
integration support, and increasing the importance of spares and
services as a competitive advantage for semiconductor equipment
manufacturers. In addition, key integrated circuit manufacturers
have become influential in technology decisions made by their
global partners. If Applied does not successfully manage the
risks resulting from the ongoing changes occurring in the
semiconductor industry, its business, financial condition and
results of operation could be materially and adversely affected.
</FONT>

<DIV>&nbsp;</DIV>

<TABLE width="100%" border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0">

<TR>
    <TD width="3%"></TD>
    <TD width="97%"></TD>
</TR>

    success.</FONT></I></B></TD>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied&#146;s success depends in large part on
its ability to attract, retain and motivate key employees,
including those in managerial, technical, marketing and support
roles. Achieving this objective may be difficult due to changes
in the global economy, the recent industry downturn,
Applied&#146;s workforce actions and realignment activities, and
its changes in management. If Applied does not successfully
attract, retain and
</FONT>

operating results and ability to capitalize on its opportunities
may be materially and adversely affected.

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied&#146;s business depends on its ability to
supply products that meet the rapidly changing demands of its
customers, which depends in part on the timely delivery of
parts, components, and subassemblies (collectively parts) from
suppliers. Some key parts may be obtained only from a single
supplier or a limited group of suppliers. In addition, Applied
outsources certain manufacturing activities. Significant
interruptions of manufacturing operations as a result of the
failure or inability of suppliers to timely deliver quality
parts, outsourcing difficulties, natural disasters (such as
earthquakes or tornadoes), or other causes (such as information
technology or infrastructure failures, regional economic
downturns, political instability, terrorism, acts of war, or
epidemics) could result in delayed product deliveries or
manufacturing inefficiencies. Any or all of these factors could
materially and adversely affect Applied&#146;s business,
financial condition and results of operations.

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied has made, and may in the future make,
acquisitions of, or significant investments in, businesses with
complementary products, services and/or technologies.
Acquisitions involve numerous risks, including but not limited
to: 1)&nbsp;diversion of management&#146;s attention from other
operational matters; 2)&nbsp;the inability to realize expected
synergies resulting from the acquisition; 3)&nbsp;failure to
commercialize purchased technology; and 4)&nbsp;impairment of
acquired intangible assets as a result of technological
advancements or worse-than-expected performance of the acquired
company. Mergers and acquisitions are inherently subject to
multiple significant risks, and the inability to effectively
manage these risks could materially and adversely affect
Applied&#146;s business, financial condition and results of
operations.

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied is subject to various risks related to:
1)&nbsp;new, different, inconsistent or even conflicting laws,
rules and regulations that may be enacted by legislative bodies
and/or regulatory agencies in the regions in which Applied
operates and with which Applied must comply; and
2)&nbsp;disagreements or disputes between national or regional
regulatory agencies related to international trade.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">For example, the World Trade Organization
(WTO)&nbsp;has determined that the U.S.&nbsp;Foreign Sales
Corporation (FSC) and Extraterritorial Income (ETI) exclusion
constitute prohibited export subsidies warranting the possible
imposition of trade sanctions on certain goods. Applied has
benefited from FSC and ETI tax provisions, and the elimination
of these tax benefits could materially and adversely affect
Applied&#146;s financial condition and results of operations.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">During fiscal 2002, Applied filed an application
with the SEC for an exemptive order confirming that it is not
subject to the Investment Company Act of 1940 (the Act), which
requires companies primarily engaged in the business of
investing in securities to comply with additional rules and
regulations. Largely due to the industry downturn,
Applied&#146;s ratios of investments to total assets and of
interest income to net income have increased, resulting in the
risk that Applied could be deemed to be covered by the Act. If
the SEC does not grant the exemption, Applied may have to take
other actions that could adversely affect its results of
operations in order not to be subject to the Act.
</FONT>

liabilities, the imposition of fines and/or the suspension or
termination of development, manufacture or use of certain of its
products, each of which could have a material adverse effect on
Applied&#146;s business, financial condition and results of
operations.

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied from time to time is, and in the future
may be, involved in legal proceedings or claims regarding patent
infringement, intellectual property rights, antitrust,
environmental regulations, securities, contracts, product
performance, employment and other matters (see Part&nbsp;II
below). In addition, Applied on occasion receives notification
from customers who believe that Applied owes them
indemnification or other obligations related to infringement
claims made against the customers by third parties. These legal
proceedings and claims, whether with or without merit, are
time-consuming and expensive to prosecute or defend and divert
management&#146;s attention and resources. There can be no
assurance regarding the outcome of current or future legal
proceedings or claims. In addition, Applied&#146;s intellectual
property rights may not provide significant competitive
advantages if they are circumvented, invalidated or rendered
obsolete by the rapid pace of technological change. Furthermore,
the laws of other countries permit the protection of
Applied&#146;s proprietary rights to varying extents, compared
to U.S. laws. Applied&#146;s success is dependent in part upon
the protection of its intellectual property rights. Infringement
of Applied&#146;s rights by a third party could result in
uncompensated lost market and revenue opportunities for Applied.
If Applied is not able to resolve a claim, negotiate a
settlement of the matter, obtain necessary licenses on
commercially reasonable terms, and/or successfully prosecute or
defend its position, Applied&#146;s business, financial
condition and results of operations could be materially and
adversely affected.
</FONT>

<!-- link2 "Item 3. Quantitative and Qualitative Disclosures About Market Risk" -->
<DIV align="left"><A NAME="003"></A></DIV>

<DIV>&nbsp;</DIV>

<TABLE width="100%" border="0" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0">

<TR>
    <TD width="8%"></TD>
    <TD width="92%"></TD>
</TR>

<TR valign="top">
    <TD><B><FONT size="2">Item&nbsp;3.</FONT></B></TD>
    <TD>
    <B><I><FONT size="2">Quantitative and Qualitative Disclosures
    About Market Risk</FONT></I></B></TD>
</TR>

</TABLE>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied purchases forward exchange and currency
option contracts to hedge certain existing and anticipated
foreign currency denominated transactions expected to occur
during the next year. Gains and losses on these contracts are
generally recognized in income when the related transactions
being hedged are recognized. Because the effect of movements in
currency exchange rates on forward exchange and currency option
contracts generally offsets the related effect on the underlying
items being hedged, these financial instruments are not expected
to subject Applied to risks that would otherwise result from
changes in currency exchange rates. Net foreign currency gains
and losses were not material for the three or nine months ended
July&nbsp;27, 2003.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">Applied has performed an analysis to assess the
potential financial effect of reasonably possible near-term
changes in interest and foreign currency exchange rates. Based
upon Applied&#146;s analysis, the effect of such rate changes is
not expected to be material to Applied&#146;s financial
condition, results of operations or cash flows.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">As required by Rule&nbsp;13a-15(b), Applied
management, including the Chief Executive Officer and Chief
Financial Officer, conducted an evaluation as of the end of the
period covered by this report, of the effectiveness of
Applied&#146;s disclosure controls and procedures as defined in
Exchange Act Rule&nbsp;13a-15(e). Based on that evaluation, the
Chief Executive Officer and Chief Financial Officer concluded
that Applied&#146;s disclosure controls and procedures were
effective as of the end of the period covered by this report. As
required by Rule&nbsp;13a-15(d), Applied management, including
the Chief Executive Officer and Chief Financial Officer, also
conducted an evaluation of Applied&#146;s internal control over
financial reporting to determine whether any changes occurred
during the quarter covered by this report that have materially
affected, or are reasonably likely to materially affect,
Applied&#146;s internal control over financial reporting. Based
on that evaluation, there has been no such change during the
quarter covered by this report.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">It should be noted that any system of controls,
however well designed and operated, can provide only reasonable,
and not absolute, assurance that the objectives of the system
will be met. In addition, the design of any control system is
based in part upon certain assumptions about the likelihood of
future events.

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">Novellus</FONT></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">On June&nbsp;13, 1997, after Varian Associates,
Inc. (Varian) failed to respond to requests by Applied to
discuss certain patent issues, Applied filed a lawsuit against
Varian captioned Applied Materials, Inc.&nbsp;v. Varian
Associates, Inc. (case no.&nbsp;C-97-20523-RMW) in the United
States District Court for the Northern District of California,
alleging infringement of several of Applied&#146;s patents
concerning PVD technology. On July&nbsp;7, 1997, Applied amended
that action to allege infringement of those same Applied PVD
patents against Novellus Systems, Inc. (Novellus) and to add
Novellus as a defendant, as a result of Novellus&#146;
acquisition of Varian&#146;s thin film systems PVD business. On
June&nbsp;23, 1997, Novellus filed a separate lawsuit against
Applied captioned Novellus Systems, Inc.&nbsp;v. Applied
Materials, Inc. (case no.&nbsp;C-97-20551-EAI) in the United
States District Court for the Northern District of California,
alleging infringement by Applied of several PVD technology
patents that were formerly owned by Varian. Novellus seeks
damages for past infringement, a permanent injunction, treble
damages for willful infringement, pre-judgment interest and
attorneys&#146; fees. In September 2000, Applied and Varian
settled their disputes, and on October&nbsp;3, 2000,
Applied&#146;s claims against Varian and Varian&#146;s claims
and counterclaims against Applied were dismissed with prejudice
with respect to the Inova system as it was made and sold as of
May&nbsp;7, 1997. The litigation with Novellus continues.
Discovery has closed in the actions. The trial date of
May&nbsp;27, 2003 has been rescheduled to January&nbsp;20, 2004.
Applied believes it has meritorious claims and defenses and
intends to pursue them vigorously.
</FONT>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">Axcelis Technologies</FONT></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">On January&nbsp;8, 2001, Axcelis Technologies,
Inc. (Axcelis), formerly a subsidiary of Eaton Corporation,
filed a lawsuit in the United States District Court for the
District of Massachusetts, captioned Axcelis Technologies,
Inc.&nbsp;v. Applied Materials, Inc. (case no.&nbsp;01-10029
DPW). The lawsuit alleges that Applied infringes a patent
concerning ion implantation owned by Axcelis. The complaint also
alleges various Massachusetts state and common law tortious
interference and unfair competition claims. Axcelis seeks a
preliminary and permanent injunction, damages, costs and
attorneys&#146; fees. On April&nbsp;12, 2001, Applied answered
the complaint by denying all allegations and counterclaimed for
declaratory judgment of invalidity and non-infringement, and
violations of various unfair and deceptive trade practices laws.
Applied seeks damages, a permanent injunction, costs and
attorneys&#146; fees. On July&nbsp;2, 2003, a jury ruled in
favor of Applied, returning a verdict that Applied&#146;s
Swift<SUP>TM</SUP> ion implantation system does not infringe
Axcelis&#146; patent. The court has entered judgment in favor of
Applied on Axcelis&#146; patent claim. Axcelis has filed a
notice of appeal. The state law claims have not been resolved.
Applied believes it has meritorious defenses and counterclaims
to the action and intends to pursue them vigorously.
</FONT>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">Linear Technology</FONT></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">On March&nbsp;2, 2001, Linear Technology Corp.
(LTC) filed a third party complaint against Applied in the
United States District Court for the Eastern District of Texas,
captioned Texas Instruments, Inc.&nbsp;v. Linear Technology
Corp.&nbsp;v. Applied Materials, Inc. (case no.&nbsp;2-01-CV4
(DF)). The complaint against Applied alleged that Applied is
obligated to indemnify LTC and defend LTC for certain claims in
the underlying patent infringement lawsuit brought by Texas
Instruments, Inc. (TI) against LTC. The complaint also alleged
claims for breach of contract, breach of warranty, and various
unfair business practices. In the complaint, LTC alleged that,
before LTC purchased certain equipment from Applied, Applied
failed to disclose to LTC that TI previously had won a jury
verdict against Hyundai Electronics Industries Co., Ltd.
(Hyundai) for patent infringement based on Hyundai&#146;s use of
certain semiconductor equipment including some Applied tools.
LTC&#146;s Texas lawsuit against Applied sought indemnification
and damages from Applied and an order requiring Applied to
defend LTC in the underlying lawsuit with TI. On
January&nbsp;15, 2002, the Court granted TI&#146;s motion to
sever Applied and the other third party defendants from the
action and dismissed LTC&#146;s action against Applied and the
other third party defendants without prejudice. On
March&nbsp;12, 2002, LTC filed a complaint against Applied in
the Superior Court for the County of Santa Clara, captioned
Linear Technology
</FONT>

<P align="center"><FONT size="2">22
</FONT>

<P><HR noshade><P>
<H5 align="left" style="page-break-before:always"><A HREF="#toc">Table of Contents</A></H5><P>

<DIV align="left">
<FONT size="2">Corp.&nbsp;v. Applied Materials, Inc., Novellus
Systems, Inc. and Tokyo Electron Ltd., (case no.&nbsp;CV806004)
alleging claims for breach of contract, fraud and deceit,
negligent misrepresentation, suppression of fact, unfair
competition, breach of warranty, express contractual indemnity,
implied equitable indemnity and declaratory relief. On
November&nbsp;12, 2002, LTC filed an amended complaint in the
Santa Clara action asserting essentially the same claims as in
the original complaint but adding an additional assertion that
LTC and TI have settled their litigation. In the amended
complaint, LTC seeks damages, punitive damages, injunctive
relief and restitution. LTC also seeks costs and attorneys&#146;
fees including costs and attorneys&#146; fees for the TI
litigation. LTC has also asserted similar claims against certain
other semiconductor equipment manufacturers. Applied&#146;s
motion to dismiss the amended complaint was granted in part. LTC
has filed a Second Amended Complaint and Applied&#146;s motion
to dismiss the Second Amended Complaint was also granted, with
leave for LTC to file a Third Amended Complaint. Applied
believes that it has meritorious defenses and intends to pursue
them vigorously.

</DIV>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">Semitool</FONT></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">On June&nbsp;11, 2001, Semitool, Inc. (Semitool)
filed a lawsuit against Applied in the United States District
Court for the Northern District of California, captioned
Semitool, Inc. v. Applied Materials, Inc. (case
no.&nbsp;CV-01-2277&nbsp;CRB). The lawsuit alleged that Applied
infringed a patent concerning seed repair and electroplating
owned by Semitool. Semitool sought a preliminary and permanent
injunction, damages, costs and attorneys&#146; fees. On
July&nbsp;12, 2001, before Applied had answered the complaint,
Semitool voluntarily dismissed its action against Applied in the
Northern District of California. On the same day, Semitool filed
a substantially identical action against Applied in the United
States District Court for the District of Oregon captioned
Semitool, Inc. v. Applied Materials, Inc. (case
no.&nbsp;CV&#146;01-1066&nbsp;AS). On July&nbsp;13, 2001,
Applied filed a declaratory judgment action against Semitool in
the Northern District of California captioned Applied Materials,
Inc. v. Semitool, Inc. (case no. CV-01-2673&nbsp;BZ). In that
action, Applied seeks a declaration that Applied has not
infringed the Semitool patent and that Semitool&#146;s patent is
invalid and unenforceable. Applied also seeks costs and
attorneys&#146; fees. The California Court has ordered
Applied&#146;s action against Semitool transferred to the
District of Oregon. The actions are proceeding together in
Oregon. Semitool has also asserted similar claims against
certain other semiconductor equipment manufacturers. The Oregon
Court has issued an order interpreting the patent claims and has
rescheduled the trial date from June&nbsp;30, 2003 to
February&nbsp;3, 2004. Applied believes it has meritorious
claims and defenses and intends to pursue them vigorously.
</FONT>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">David Scharf</FONT></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">On July&nbsp;31, 2001, David Scharf, an
individual, filed a lawsuit against Applied in the United States
District Court for the Central District of California, captioned
David Scharf v. Applied Materials, Inc. (case
no.&nbsp;01-06580&nbsp;AHM). The lawsuit alleges that Applied
has infringed, has induced others to infringe and has
contributed to others&#146; infringement of a patent concerning
color synthesizing scanning electron microscope technology.
Mr.&nbsp;Scharf seeks a preliminary and permanent injunction,
damages and costs. Applied has answered the complaint and
counterclaimed for declaratory judgment of non-infringement and
invalidity. On May&nbsp;10, 2002, Mr.&nbsp;Scharf filed a
request for re-examination of his own patent. On June&nbsp;26,
2002, the case was removed from the Court&#146;s active docket
after the parties stipulated to stay the case pending the
results of that re-examination. On July&nbsp;11, 2002, Applied
filed its own request for re-examination of
Mr.&nbsp;Scharf&#146;s patent with the Patent and Trademark
Office. Applied&#146;s request for re-examination was granted on
September&nbsp;19, 2002. Applied believes it has meritorious
defenses and counterclaims and intends to pursue them vigorously.
</FONT>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">ASMI</FONT></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">On August&nbsp;27, 2002, ASM America, Inc. and
ASM International, N.V. (collectively ASMI) filed a lawsuit
against Applied in the United States District Court for the
District of Arizona, captioned ASM America, Inc. and ASM
International, N.V. v. Applied Materials, Inc. (case
no.&nbsp;Civ&#146;02&nbsp;1660&nbsp;PHX&nbsp;SMM). The lawsuit
seeks a judicial declaration that ASMI does not infringe six
patents belonging to Applied that relate to remote cleaning of
CVD chambers and to deposition of silicon nitride. The suit also
seeks a judicial
</FONT>

<P align="center"><FONT size="2">23
</FONT>

<!-- PAGEBREAK -->

<DIV align="left">
<FONT size="2">declaration that two of those six patents are
invalid. On December&nbsp;16, 2002, Applied responded to the
complaint by denying the allegations and counterclaiming for
infringement of two of the patents related to remote cleaning of
CVD chambers. Applied seeks damages, a preliminary and permanent
injunction, costs and attorneys&#146; fees. Applied also moved
to dismiss the complaint with respect to four of the patents and
moved for a more definitive statement with respect to two of
ASMI&#146;s causes of action. ASMI moved for summary judgment
that it has not infringed the same four patents that are the
subject of Applied&#146;s motion to dismiss. On July&nbsp;7,
2003, the Court denied Applied&#146;s motion to dismiss four of
the patents and also denied ASMI&#146;s summary judgment motion.
No trial date has been set. Applied believes it has meritorious
defenses and counterclaims and intends to pursue them vigorously.
</FONT>
</DIV>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">Varian Semiconductor Equipment Associates,
Inc.</FONT></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">On September&nbsp;13, 2002, Varian Semiconductor
Equipment Associates, Inc. filed a demand for arbitration with
the American Arbitration Association asserting that Applied has
breached a patent license agreement between Varian and Applied
dated January&nbsp;1, 1992. Varian seeks to recover royalties,
interest and attorneys&#146; fees. The arbitration hearing on
whether the products are covered by the license agreement has
concluded. On May&nbsp;2, 2003, the arbitration panel issued an
interim decision finding that some, but not all, of the products
at issue were subject to the agreement. The arbitration panel
next will consider whether the asserted claims of the patents
under which those products were found to be covered are valid.
Applied believes that it has meritorious defenses and intends to
pursue them vigorously.
</FONT>

<P align="left">
<B><FONT size="2">Robert Bosch GmbH</FONT></B>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">On October&nbsp;10, 2002, Robert Bosch GmbH
(Bosch), a German company, filed a lawsuit against Applied in
the United States District Court for the District of Delaware,
captioned Robert Bosch GmbH&nbsp;v. Applied Materials, Inc.
(civil action no. &nbsp;02-1523). The lawsuit alleges that
Applied infringes two patents owned by Bosch related to
anisotrophic etching. Bosch seeks a preliminary and permanent
injunction, damages, costs and attorneys&#146; fees. Applied has
answered the complaint and counterclaimed for declaratory
judgment of non-infringement and invalidity. No trial date has
been set. Applied believes it has meritorious defenses and
intends to pursue them vigorously.
</FONT>

<P align="left">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;
<FONT size="2">From time to time, Applied receives notification
from customers claiming that such customers are entitled to
indemnification or other obligations from Applied related to
infringement claims made against the customers by third parties.
In addition, Applied is subject to various other legal
proceedings and claims, either asserted or unasserted, that
arise in the ordinary course of business. Although the outcome
of these claims cannot be predicted with certainty, Applied does
not believe that any of these other existing legal matters will
have a material adverse effect on its financial condition or
results of operations.
</FONT>

<H5 align="left" style="page-break-before:always">&nbsp;</H5><P>
<P align="right"><FONT size="2">EXHIBIT 31.1</FONT>


<P align="center"><FONT size="2"><B>CERTIFICATION</B>
</FONT>

</FONT>

complies with the requirements of section 13(a) or 15(d) of the

    <TD width="96%"><FONT size="2">the information contained in this Form&nbsp;10-Q for the period
ended July&nbsp;27, 2003 fairly presents, in all material respects,
